Garth Brooks being the #1 best selling solo artist is technically true, but also incredibly misleading. Wal Mart sold a bundle of all of his albums for like $30, which even back in the 90s and early 00s was a crazy good deal. The misleading thing is that for each bundle sold, they counted it as like 8 album sales, which grossly inflated his numbers.  If you disregard the bundle sales (or count them as one album), I doubt he'd even be in the top 10.

Itâ€™s amazing how much is misunderstood when looking back on a world you never lived in. Dannyâ€™s life, although born in the 90s would first be shaped like many his age by the events of September 11th, 2001. The world that shaped him and the only world he knew was the post-9/11 world. A world that would be introduced to Web 2.0 only a few years after. Essentially, the world Danny tries to understand is one very different from the world he knows and heâ€™s always lived in. In 1999, Google barely existed, just one of many other search engines on the Internet; YouTube didnâ€™t exist; and social media was a concept that wouldnâ€™t even begin to form for another 4 years with the introduction of MySpace and Facebook just a year later. Chris Gaines was a massive marketing campaign, an ARG before the term was even coined. Although brilliant, it was a bit ahead of its time and hence misunderstood by many. The technology and the mediums that would make ARGs really successful were still years away, but I applaud the vision and effort that went wholeheartedly into creating Chris Gaines and the lore behind him.

I remember Stephen King had to go under a pseudonym for a while. He was churning out so many books but was only allowed to publish one per year, which was standard for authors at the time. Since he wanted to get his stories out there but wasnâ€™t allowed to do it under his own name in fear of â€œover-saturating the market with the King nameâ€, He had to go by Richard Bachman for any second releases in one year. Under this name he wrote one of, if not the most controversial piece in his bibliography, â€œRageâ€, which has been attributed to inspiring at least three separate school shootings.   He later â€œkilledâ€ Richard Bachman when views around publishing numbers changed and he was allowed to have more than one book a year, only then admitting it was him who wrote the books (though it had been theorised before he confirmed it). He wanted to see if his success was luck or talent, but was outed as Bachman too soon to tell in his opinion, though receptions of the books were still high on release, before Kings name was attached.   This feels like something similar. The brand wouldnâ€™t let him just switch styles and confuse people, so he tried a pseudonym.

I was in college when this happened. You have to remember the internet wasn't full of news articles or social media or search engines like it is now. We had MTv.  That music video was ğŸ’¯ released as part of a publicity stunt (most likely to see if he could infiltrate both the pop & country charts the way Canadian Shania Twain did in America). But he knew he couldn't do it with his cowboy look and he didn't want that yeehaw persona to affect the experiment because everyone literally associated him as THE face of country. They released "Lost in You" on MTV in July and there was ZERO mentioning of "Garth Brooks" on the album name because he absolutely WAS trying to trick everyone. Everyone immediately figured it out and were really weirded out by it because everyone thought he was just being a liar and a poser. (YOU DID NOT WANT TO BE A POSER) The public didn't appreciate trying to be fooled, especially with no context. Thennnnnnn, in the fall, after a lot of weird reactions, his real name was suddenly on the album name and it was supposedly for some movie but we all knew it was a BS PR experiment gone wrong. And then it faded away with his relevancy. It was really, really bizarre and not in the provocative way. Just wanted to give a perspective from someone who was like WTF when it happened. Also, you need to watch Wayne's World. "Party on Garth!" ğŸ˜‚

14:56 they weren't googling it. ğŸ˜…
From someone who was around at the time, you have to understand some things: Brooks was the hottest thing in music for a brief period (and yes, it was weird having a country artist be the hottest thing in music). His label was looking for ways to cash in on his popularity and settled for trying to get him into the movies. He wanted to play a "rock star" so it was going to be a fictional biopic for this character. At no point was anyone trying to fool anyone into thinking Chris Gaines was a different person, and in fact the label was worried his fans *would* think that, hence the awkward addition of "Garth Brooks As..." on the title of the album (keep in mind, the internet wasn't a big thing at the time). At the same time, Brooks wanted to play it straight when he performed as Chris Gaines, which makes it seem in retrospect like he was trying to fool people.  The problem wound up being that the character and the songs didn't appeal to his fan base (or anyone else) and the movie project, which wasn't widespread knowledge, never got off the ground so people had no idea what the impetus for it was.

Okay: a lot of information to unpack.  The part of me that confuses me the most about the video is you didnâ€™t simply ask people what they remember instead relied on wiki and a handful of articles.  What that lacks is all the build up they did on television leading up to this.  The VH1 behind the music was simply one thing of hundreds of news stories, morning show appearances, radio interviews on rock and country stations.  As a Garth Brooks fan at the time that album was released I do remember the excitement turned confusion, to simply uninterested in it after listening to it.   First things first he was open that Chris Gained was fake, but he said he wanted to explore darker art than his fans were use to and one artist in particular reminded him there are ways to reinvent yourself without actually changing your original persona.   David Bowie, more so than even Madonna one artist has changed his style even adopted different personalities on stage and that is and was David Bowie. Who famously had his Ziggy stardust era, then pivoted through styles faster than Usain Bolt at olympic trials.   And as Garth Brooks was selling out arenaâ€™s in the mid 90s so was Bowie who had just a year or so prior had done a experimental industrial rock (See Nine inch nails) album and tour called outside.  The album was much like if Twin peaks soundtrack and story was rewritten by Trent Reznor and sung by Bowie. My pet theory is that the Chris Gaines 'project' was just a clever cover for something that would have been even more damaging to Garth's career.  I think he WAS going to make The Lamb, but it was an idea pitched to him by some Evangelicals wanting to make a movie, and it was going to be an outright movie-length Chick Tract about Chris Gaines the troubled, sex addicted rock star finding Jesus. Then almost immediately, people around Garth were like "Yo dude you CANNOT make that, I know it sounds like a great idea but your religion isn't a huge part of your image and you're going to look like a joke to lot of fans who aren't Evangelicals." So Garth had to backpedal like hell and make it all look like some elaborate stunt to hide one move that was much more straightforward but way more embarrassing.

I was in college when this happened, and I never got the impression he was serious about "hiding" that Gaines was really Garth Brooks. It was very much done with a wink. Also, it's really hard to communicate just how huge Garth Brooks was at the time. He was unquestionably the biggest musician in the nation, and, almost single-handedly, brought country music back into the main stream. It also bears mentioning that this was the era of Nine Inch Nails, Nirvana, all these really edgy musicians. Garth brooks had this really wholesome image, and I think, playing this character who has a way to escape from his own image without actually abandoning an insanely successful career entirely.

As Garth Brooks, his career skyrocketed, but Chris's passion as a rockster still burns inside, believing that he can make it work as his true self and not having to hide behind the Garth Brooks facade anymore. So I've done a lot of research into the Chris Gaines phenomenon and... I just want to clear up a few confusing tidbits. First off, the album was originally released as "Chris Gaines Greatest Hits". His backstory includes being in the band "Crush" (he and his bandmate wanted to be the new Lennon and McCartney) and then going on to do some solo projects. Another important tidbit is that while he was doing this stunt in 1999, Garth Brooks and his then wife were separated, with his divorce happening a few years later in 2001. With that context in mind, his VH1 Behind the Life documentary is a WILD ride. The amount of time spent discussing his sex addiction is insane and plays out like what a middle schooler thinks sex addiction would be. Pair that with the fact that he was separating from his wife and it looks like he's severely overcompensating with a character that HE CREATED by making Chris Gaines a rocker sex god. For more context, I made a helpful video analysis: https://youtu.be/rC-VjV8cDDw

Personally, as a huge Garth Brooks fan at the time, I thought it was cool and a very interesting idea.  I was interested in seeing the movie, but the public didn't like the "bit" so no movie came out. As a fan, reading all the stuff I could, there was no "trick." He wasn't trying to fool people. He was so stuck in country and there were very strict lines in music. People didn't just change genres. It was a way to do some different music without it being tied to the Garth Brooks IP. It wasn't as crazy as people are making it out to be. I was around 25 at the time. I thought it was cool that they tried to do this whole backstory and everything. I understood what he was doing. I guess no one else did.

So I've done a lot of research into the Chris Gaines phenomenon and... I just want to clear up a few confusing tidbits. First off, the album was originally released as "Chris Gaines Greatest Hits". His backstory includes being in the band "Crush" (he and his bandmate wanted to be the new Lennon and McCartney) and then going on to do some solo projects. Another important tidbit is that while he was doing this stunt in 1999, Garth Brooks and his then wife were separated, with his divorce happening a few years later in 2001. With that context in mind, his VH1 Behind the Life documentary is a WILD ride. The amount of time spent discussing his sex addiction is insane and plays out like what a middle schooler thinks sex addiction would be. Pair that with the fact that he was separating from his wife and it looks like he's severely overcompensating with a character that HE CREATED by making Chris Gaines a rocker sex god. For more context, I made a helpful video analysis: https://youtu.be/rC-VjV8cDDw

To be honest, this wasn't an entirely new thing Garth did. Back in the late 1930's, Hank Williams Sr. did the same thing. He created Luke the Drifter as a way of being able to release some of his music that had darker tones to it as he wanted to be able to release those songs to the public, but he didn't want such dark songs to be associated with Hank Williams (since Hank tried to keep his music more upbeat).  No one knew that Hank Williams and Luke the Drifter were the same person until well after Hank's death.

I actually have a story that's a little similar to this. It's the story of the song 45 RPM by this Welsh band called The Alarm, that I grew up listening to and seeing live very often because of my mum. I'm going to talk about the story below, but there's actually a movie inspired by those events called Vinyl - it's a good watch, honestly, if you're into your comedy with an edge of 'kitchen sink' realism. The story goes, The Alarm had been broken up for about a decade before they tried to get their new music played on radio stations in the mid-2000s, but were turned away because they were considered 'too old' having been around and making music since the 80s. So what did they do? They recorded a new song under a different band name (The Poppy Fields), hired a bunch of young musicians to pose as the band, and then had that 'new band of hip young things' try and get their music on air. The wildest part of the story...? It ACTUALLY worked, and The Alarm had to step in and own up to it. Apparently some DJs in the UK from around then STILL refuse to play any of their songs after that stunt. XD It's honestly one of my fave songs by them as well, I used to dance like crazy when they played it live in my childhood and teens.

The thing about musical artists is that they also enjoy a wide variety of music, just like anyone else, but they're essentially forced into picking A Sound that becomes the only thing people want to hear from them, and is therefore the only thing their producers and labels will let them sing. So it's not uncommon for them to get really bizarre in their attempts to break out and try something new. Sometimes it's forgotten about, sometimes it becomes a meme, and sometimes they genuinely completely change their music and persona and people start coming up with conspiracy theories about them having died and replaced with a body double.

There was a really big popstar in Germany in the 90s named Sasha. At the beginning of the 00s, he took a break from his musical career and a few years later a band called "Dick Brave and the backbeats" showed up. They made rockabilly inspired music (completely different from Sasha) and Dick Brave (who had a plausible biography) said he was Canadian and had a thick North American accent when speaking German. He never broke character and would invent stuff when being asked something in interviews.  I think it was never made official that Sasha was Dick Brave and it took years for the public to notice it even though it was rumored for quite a while, but it was funny how much lore they made up for it and how plausible it seemed in the early 00s.

I'm in my 40's, and was (and am) a Garth Brooks fan. I picked up the Chris Gaines album without realizing it was Garth - it was fine, not anything to write home about, but imagine my surprise when I later on figured out they were the same musician lol. Garth was at the height of popularity at that point, and it wasn't that strange to me that he would do this - it felt like Stephen King writing as Richard Bachman - just a way to create something that didn't have to be associated with his name, fame and the stigma of country music (which is a very real thing). It's also a very different sound from his regular music, like Bachman's books felt more violent and "raw" than King's books at the time - I think it gave him a chance to explore music from a different angle than he could as a superstar with a deeply established brand.

Chocolate chip cookies   Ingredients (for 12 cookies)  Â½ cup (100 g) sugar Â¾ cup (165 g) brown sugar, packed 1 teaspoon salt Â½ cup (115 g) unsalted butter, melted 1 egg 1 teaspoon vanilla extract 1 Â¼ cups (155 g) all-purpose flour Â½ teaspoon baking soda 4 oz (110 g) semi-sweet chocolate chunk, or milk chocolate 4 oz (110 g) dark chocolate chunk, or your preference whole milk, to drink, optional  Preparation 1. In a large bowl, whisk together the sugars, salt, and butter until a paste has formed with no lumps. 2. Whisk in the egg and vanilla, beating until light ribbons fall off the whisk. 3. Sift in the flour and baking soda, then fold the mixture with a spatula, being careful not to overmix too much. This will cause the gluten in the flour to toughen which will result in cakier cookies. 4. Fold in the chocolate chunks evenly, then chill the dough for at least 30 minutes. For a more intense toffee-like flavor and deeper color, chill the dough overnight. The longer the dough rests, the more complex its flavor will be. 5. Preheat oven to 350Â°F (180Â°C). 6. Scoop the dough with an ice-cream scoop onto a parchment paper-lined baking sheet, leaving at least 4 inches (10 cm) of space between cookies and 2 inches (5 cm) of space from the edges of the pan so that the cookies can spread evenly. 7. Bake for 12-15 minutes, or until the edges have started to barely brown. 8. Cool completely, and serve yourself a glass of milk. 9. Enjoy.
